      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latvia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <p>The name <i>Latvija</i> is derived from the name of the ancient <a href="/wiki/Latgalians" title="Latgalians">Latgalians</a>, one of four <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-Europeans" title="Proto-Indo-Europeans">Indo-European</a> <a href="/wiki/Balts" title="Balts">Baltic tribes</a> (along with <a href="/wiki/Couronians" class="mw-redirect" title="Couronians">Couronians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Selonians" title="Selonians">Selonians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Semigallians" title="Semigallians">Semigallians</a>), which formed the ethnic core of modern <a href="/wiki/Latvian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Latvian people">Latvians</a> together with the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Finns" class="mw-redirect" title="Baltic Finns">Finnic</a> <a href="/wiki/Livonian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Livonian people">Livonians</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_of_Latvia" title="Henry of Latvia">Henry of Latvia</a> coined the <a href="/wiki/Latinisation_of_names" title="Latinisation of names">latinisations</a> of the country's name, "Lettigallia" and "Lethia", both derived from the Latgalians. The terms inspired the variations on the country's name in <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a> from "Letonia" and in several <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a> from "Lettland".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latvia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Latvia" title="History of Latvia">History of Latvia</a>
      </div> 
      <p>Around 3000 BC, the proto-Baltic ancestors of the Latvian people settled on the eastern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic Sea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Balts" title="Balts">Balts</a> established trade routes to Rome and <a href="/wiki/Byzantium" title="Byzantium">Byzantium</a>, trading local <a href="/wiki/Amber" title="Amber">amber</a> for precious metals.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> By 900 AD, four distinct Baltic tribes inhabited Latvia: <a href="/wiki/Curonians" title="Curonians">Curonians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latgalians" title="Latgalians">Latgalians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Selonians" title="Selonians">Selonians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Semigallians" title="Semigallians">Semigallians</a> (in Latvian: <i>kurši</i>, <i>latgaļi</i>, <i>sēļi</i> and <i>zemgaļi</i>), as well as the Finnic tribe of <a href="/wiki/Livonian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Livonian people">Livonians</a> (<i>lībieši</i>) speaking a Finnic language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>In the 12th century in the territory of Latvia, there were lands with their rulers: <a href="/w/index.php?title=Vanema&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Vanema (page does not exist)">Vanema</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ventava" class="mw-redirect" title="Ventava">Ventava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bandava" title="Bandava">Bandava</a>, <a href="/wiki/Piemare" title="Piemare">Piemare</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Duvzare&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Duvzare (page does not exist)">Duvzare</a>, <a href="/w/index.php?title=Upmale&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Upmale (page does not exist)">Upmale</a>, <a href="/wiki/S%C4%93lija" class="mw-redirect" title="Sēlija">Sēlija</a>, <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Koknese" title="Principality of Koknese">Koknese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jersika" class="mw-redirect" title="Jersika">Jersika</a>, <a href="/wiki/T%C4%81lava" title="Tālava">Tālava</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=Adzele&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Adzele (page does not exist)">Adzele</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_period">Medieval period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latvia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Medieval period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Terra_Mariana" title="Terra Mariana">Terra Mariana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Livonian_Crusade" title="Livonian Crusade">Livonian Crusade</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Crusades" title="Northern Crusades">Northern Crusades</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tleft">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Livonia_1260.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Medieval_Livonia_1260.svg/170px-Medieval_Livonia_1260.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Medieval_Livonia_1260.svg/255px-Medieval_Livonia_1260.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Medieval_Livonia_1260.svg/340px-Medieval_Livonia_1260.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="955" data-file-height="1034"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Livonia_1260.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Terra_Mariana" title="Terra Mariana">Terra Mariana</a>, medieval Livonia
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Although the local people had contact with the outside world for centuries, they became more fully integrated into the European socio-political system in the 12th century.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The first missionaries, sent by the Pope, sailed up the <a href="/wiki/Daugava_River" class="mw-redirect" title="Daugava River">Daugava River</a> in the late 12th century, seeking converts.<sup id="cite_ref-Lonely_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lonely-41">[41]</a></sup> The local people, however, did not convert to Christianity as readily as the Church had hoped.<sup id="cite_ref-Lonely_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lonely-41">[41]</a></sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" class="mw-redirect" title="Teutonic Knights">German crusaders</a> were sent, or more likely decided to go on their own accord as they were known to do. <a href="/wiki/Saint_Meinhard" title="Saint Meinhard">Saint Meinhard</a> of Segeberg arrived in Ikšķile, in 1184, traveling with merchants to Livonia, on a Catholic mission to convert the population from their original <a href="/wiki/Pagan" class="mw-redirect" title="Pagan">pagan</a> beliefs. Pope Celestine III had called for <a href="/wiki/Livonian_Crusade#Wars_against_Estonians_(1208–27)" title="Livonian Crusade">a crusade against pagans in Northern Europe</a> in 1193. When peaceful means of conversion failed to produce results, Meinhard plotted to convert Livonians by force of arms.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Burg_Turaida04.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Burg_Turaida04.jpg/220px-Burg_Turaida04.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Burg_Turaida04.jpg/330px-Burg_Turaida04.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Burg_Turaida04.jpg/440px-Burg_Turaida04.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3570" data-file-height="2665"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Burg_Turaida04.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Turaida_Castle" title="Turaida Castle">Turaida Castle</a> near <a href="/wiki/Sigulda" title="Sigulda">Sigulda</a>, built in 1214 under <a href="/wiki/Albert_of_Riga" title="Albert of Riga">Albert of Riga</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>At the beginning of the 13th century, Germans ruled large parts of what is currently Latvia.<sup id="cite_ref-Lonely_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lonely-41">[41]</a></sup> Together with southern Estonia, these conquered areas formed the <a href="/wiki/Crusader_state" class="mw-redirect" title="Crusader state">crusader state</a> that became known as <a href="/wiki/Terra_Mariana" title="Terra Mariana">Terra Mariana</a> or Livonia. In 1282, Riga, and later the cities of <a href="/wiki/C%C4%93sis" title="Cēsis">Cēsis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limba%C5%BEi" title="Limbaži">Limbaži</a>, <a href="/wiki/Koknese" title="Koknese">Koknese</a> and <a href="/wiki/Valmiera" title="Valmiera">Valmiera</a>, became part of the <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Lonely_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lonely-41">[41]</a></sup> Riga became an important point of east–west trading<sup id="cite_ref-Lonely_41-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lonely-41">[41]</a></sup> and formed close cultural links with <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The first German settlers were knights from northern Germany and citizens of northern German towns who brought their <a href="/wiki/Low_German" title="Low German">Low German</a> language to the region, which shaped many loanwords in the Latvian language.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Reformation_period_and_Polish.E2.80.93Lithuanian_rule"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Reformation_period_and_Polish–Lithuanian_rule">Reformation period and Polish–Lithuanian rule</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latvia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Reformation period and Polish–Lithuanian rule">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Livonia" title="Swedish Livonia">Swedish Livonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Courland_and_Semigallia" title="Duchy of Courland and Semigallia">Duchy of Courland and Semigallia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Livonia" title="Duchy of Livonia">Duchy of Livonia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Swedish_Empire_(1560-1815)_en2.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png/220px-Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png/330px-Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png/440px-Swedish_Empire_%281560-1815%29_en2.png 2x" data-file-width="1802" data-file-height="2213"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Swedish_Empire_(1560-1815)_en2.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>The <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Empire" title="Swedish Empire">Swedish Empire</a> (1560–1815).
         <br>Riga became the capital of <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Livonia" title="Swedish Livonia">Swedish Livonia</a> and the largest city in the Swedish Empire.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>After the <a href="/wiki/Livonian_War" title="Livonian War">Livonian War</a> (1558–1583), Livonia (Northern Latvia &amp; Southern Estonia) fell under <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish and Lithuanian</a> rule.<sup id="cite_ref-Lonely_41-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lonely-41">[41]</a></sup> The southern part of Estonia and the northern part of Latvia were ceded to the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a> and formed into the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Livonia" title="Duchy of Livonia">Duchy of Livonia</a> (<i>Ducatus Livoniae Ultradunensis</i>). <a href="/wiki/Gotthard_Kettler" title="Gotthard Kettler">Gotthard Kettler</a>, the last Master of the <a href="/wiki/Livonian_Order" title="Livonian Order">Order of Livonia</a>, formed the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Courland_and_Semigallia" title="Duchy of Courland and Semigallia">Duchy of Courland and Semigallia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Though the duchy was a vassal state to Poland, it retained a considerable degree of autonomy and experienced a golden age in the 16th century. <a href="/wiki/Latgalia" class="mw-redirect" title="Latgalia">Latgalia</a>, the easternmost region of Latvia, became a part of the <a href="/wiki/Inflanty_Voivodeship" title="Inflanty Voivodeship">Inflanty Voivodeship</a> of the Polish-Lithuanian Commonwealth.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 17th and early 18th centuries, the Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth, <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Empire" title="Swedish Empire">Sweden</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russia</a> struggled for supremacy in the eastern Baltic. After the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Swedish_War_(1600%E2%80%931611)" title="Polish–Swedish War (1600–1611)">Polish–Swedish War</a>, northern Livonia (including Vidzeme) came under Swedish rule. Riga became the capital of <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Livonia" title="Swedish Livonia">Swedish Livonia</a> and the largest city in the entire Swedish Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> Fighting continued sporadically between Sweden and Poland until the <a href="/wiki/Truce_of_Altmark" title="Truce of Altmark">Truce of Altmark</a> in 1629<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In Latvia, the Swedish period is generally remembered as positive; <a href="/wiki/Serfdom" title="Serfdom">serfdom</a> was eased, a network of schools was established for the peasantry, and the power of the regional <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Germans" title="Baltic Germans">barons</a> was diminished.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Several important cultural changes occurred during this time. Under Swedish and largely German rule, western Latvia adopted <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> as its main religion. The ancient tribes of the Couronians, Semigallians, Selonians, Livs, and northern Latgallians assimilated to form the <a href="/wiki/Latvian_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Latvian people">Latvian people</a>, speaking one <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian language</a>. Throughout all the centuries, however, an actual Latvian state had not been established, so the borders and definitions of who exactly fell within that group are largely subjective. Meanwhile, largely isolated from the rest of Latvia, southern Latgallians adopted <a href="/wiki/Catholicism" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a> under Polish/<a href="/wiki/Society_of_Jesus" title="Society of Jesus">Jesuit</a> influence. The native dialect remained distinct, although it acquired many Polish and Russian loanwords.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span id="Livonia_.26_Courland_in_the_Russian_Empire_.281795.E2.80.931917.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Livonia_&amp;_Courland_in_the_Russian_Empire_(1795–1917)">Livonia &amp; Courland in the Russian Empire (1795–1917)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latvia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Livonia &amp; Courland in the Russian Empire (1795–1917)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <table class="box-More_citations_needed_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
       <tbody>
        <tr>
         <td class="mbox-image">
          <div style="width:52px">
           <a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" decoding="async" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399"></a>
          </div></td>
         <td class="mbox-text">
          <div class="mbox-text-span">
           This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Latvia&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">March 2015</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small>
          </div></td>
        </tr>
       </tbody>
      </table> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Capitulation_of_Estonia_and_Livonia" title="Capitulation of Estonia and Livonia">capitulation of Estonia and Livonia</a> in 1710 and the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nystad" title="Treaty of Nystad">Treaty of Nystad</a>, ending the <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_War" title="Great Northern War">Great Northern War</a> in 1721, gave Vidzeme to Russia (it became part of the <a href="/wiki/Governorate_of_Livonia" title="Governorate of Livonia">Riga Governorate</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Latgale region remained part of the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish–Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> as <a href="/wiki/Inflanty_Voivodeship" title="Inflanty Voivodeship">Inflanty Voivodeship</a> until 1772, when it was incorporated into Russia. The <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Courland_and_Semigallia" title="Duchy of Courland and Semigallia">Duchy of Courland and Semigallia</a> became an <a href="/wiki/Autonomous_area" class="mw-redirect" title="Autonomous area">autonomous</a> Russian province (the <a href="/wiki/Courland_Governorate" title="Courland Governorate">Courland Governorate</a>) in 1795, bringing all of what is now Latvia into the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>. All three Baltic provinces preserved local laws, German as the local <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> and their own parliament, the <a href="/wiki/Landtag" title="Landtag">Landtag</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>During the <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_War" title="Great Northern War">Great Northern War</a> (1700–1721), up to 40 percent of Latvians died from famine and plague.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Half the residents of Riga were killed by <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_War_plague_outbreak" title="Great Northern War plague outbreak">plague in 1710–1711</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The emancipation of the serfs took place in Courland in 1817 and in Vidzeme in 1819.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In practice, however, the emancipation was actually advantageous to the landowners and nobility,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> as it dispossessed peasants of their land without compensation, forcing them to return to work at the estates "of their own free will".<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>During these two centuries Latvia experienced economic and construction boom – ports were expanded (Riga became the largest port in the Russian Empire), railways built; new factories, banks, and a University were established; many residential, public (theatres and museums), and school buildings were erected; new parks formed; and so on. Riga's boulevards and some streets outside the Old Town date from this period.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p><a href="/wiki/Numeracy" title="Numeracy">Numeracy</a> was also higher in the Livonian and Courlandian parts of the Russian Empire, which may have been influenced by the Protestant religion of the inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h4><span class="mw-headline" id="National_awakening">National awakening</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latvia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: National awakening">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4> 
      <p>During the 19th century, the social structure changed dramatically.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A class of independent farmers established itself after reforms allowed the peasants to repurchase their land, but many landless peasants remained.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> There also developed a growing urban <a href="/wiki/Proletariat" title="Proletariat">proletariat</a> and an increasingly influential Latvian <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeoisie</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Young_Latvians" title="Young Latvians">Young Latvian</a> (<a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian</a>: <i lang="lv">Jaunlatvieši</i>) movement laid the groundwork for nationalism from the middle of the century, many of its leaders looking to the <a href="/wiki/Slavophile" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavophile">Slavophiles</a> for support against the prevailing German-dominated social order.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The rise in use of the Latvian language in literature and society became known as the <a href="/wiki/Latvian_National_Awakening" title="Latvian National Awakening">First National Awakening</a>. <a href="/wiki/Russification" title="Russification">Russification</a> began in Latgale after the Polish led the <a href="/wiki/January_Uprising" title="January Uprising">January Uprising</a> in 1863: this spread to the rest of what is now Latvia by the 1880s.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Young Latvians were largely eclipsed by the <a href="/wiki/New_Current" title="New Current">New Current</a>, a broad leftist social and political movement, in the 1890s. Popular discontent exploded in the <a href="/wiki/1905_Russian_Revolution" title="1905 Russian Revolution">1905 Russian Revolution</a>, which took a nationalist character in the Baltic provinces.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Declaration_of_independence">Declaration of independence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latvia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Declaration of independence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Janis_Cakste.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Janis_Cakste.jpg/170px-Janis_Cakste.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="232" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Janis_Cakste.jpg/255px-Janis_Cakste.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Janis_Cakste.jpg/340px-Janis_Cakste.jpg 2x" data-file-width="373" data-file-height="508"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Janis_Cakste.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/J%C4%81nis_%C4%8Cakste" title="Jānis Čakste">Jānis Čakste</a> (1859–1927), was the first <a href="/wiki/President_of_Latvia" title="President of Latvia">president of Latvia</a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p><a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> devastated the territory of what became the state of Latvia, and other western parts of the Russian Empire. Demands for <a href="/wiki/Self-determination" title="Self-determination">self-determination</a> were initially confined to <a href="/wiki/Autonomy" title="Autonomy">autonomy</a>, until a power vacuum was created by the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution" title="Russian Revolution">Russian Revolution</a> in 1917, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Brest-Litovsk_(Russia%E2%80%93Central_Powers)" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Brest-Litovsk (Russia–Central Powers)">Treaty of Brest-Litovsk</a> between Russia and Germany in March 1918, then the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_I" title="Allies of World War I">Allied</a> armistice with Germany on 11 November 1918. On 18 November 1918, in Riga, the <a href="/wiki/Tautas_padome" class="mw-redirect" title="Tautas padome">People's Council of Latvia</a> proclaimed the independence of the new country, with <a href="/wiki/K%C4%81rlis_Ulmanis" title="Kārlis Ulmanis">Kārlis Ulmanis</a> becoming the head of the <a href="/wiki/Latvian_Provisional_Government" title="Latvian Provisional Government">provisional government</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The General representative of Germany <a href="/wiki/August_Winnig" title="August Winnig">August Winnig</a> formally handed over political power to the Latvian Provisional Government on 26 November. </p>
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Latvian_War_of_Independence" title="Latvian War of Independence">war of independence</a> that followed was part of a general chaotic period of civil and new border wars in Eastern Europe. By the spring of 1919, there were actually three governments: the Provisional government headed by <a href="/wiki/K%C4%81rlis_Ulmanis" title="Kārlis Ulmanis">Kārlis Ulmanis</a>, supported by <a href="/wiki/Tautas_padome" class="mw-redirect" title="Tautas padome">Tautas padome</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Military_Inter-Allied_Commission_of_Control" title="Military Inter-Allied Commission of Control">Inter-Allied Commission of Control</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Latvian_Socialist_Soviet_Republic" title="Latvian Socialist Soviet Republic">Latvian Soviet government</a> led by <a href="/wiki/P%C4%93teris_Stu%C4%8Dka" title="Pēteris Stučka">Pēteris Stučka</a>, supported by the <a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">Red Army</a>; and the Provisional government headed by <a href="/wiki/Andrievs_Niedra" title="Andrievs Niedra">Andrievs Niedra</a> and supported by the <a href="/wiki/Baltische_Landeswehr" title="Baltische Landeswehr">Baltische Landeswehr</a> and the German <a href="/wiki/Freikorps" title="Freikorps">Freikorps</a> unit <i><a href="/wiki/Freikorps_in_the_Baltic" title="Freikorps in the Baltic">Iron Division</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>Estonian and Latvian forces defeated the Germans at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_C%C4%93sis_(1919)" title="Battle of Cēsis (1919)">Battle of Wenden</a> in June 1919,<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> and a massive attack by a predominantly German force—the <a href="/wiki/West_Russian_Volunteer_Army" title="West Russian Volunteer Army">West Russian Volunteer Army</a>—under <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Bermondt-Avalov" title="Pavel Bermondt-Avalov">Pavel Bermondt-Avalov</a> was repelled in November. Eastern Latvia was cleared of Red Army forces by Latvian and Polish troops in early 1920 (from the Polish perspective the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Daugavpils" title="Battle of Daugavpils">Battle of Daugavpils</a> was a part of the <a href="/wiki/Polish%E2%80%93Soviet_War" title="Polish–Soviet War">Polish–Soviet War</a>).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p>
      <p>A freely elected <a href="/wiki/Constituent_assembly" title="Constituent assembly">Constituent assembly</a> convened on 1 May 1920, and adopted a liberal constitution, the <i><a href="/wiki/Constitution_of_Latvia" title="Constitution of Latvia">Satversme</a></i>, in February 1922.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The constitution was partly suspended by Kārlis Ulmanis after <a href="/wiki/1934_Latvian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1934 Latvian coup d'état">his coup in 1934</a> but reaffirmed in 1990. Since then, it has been amended and is still in effect in Latvia today. With most of Latvia's industrial base evacuated to the interior of Russia in 1915, radical <a href="/wiki/Land_reform" title="Land reform">land reform</a> was the central political question for the young state. In 1897, 61.2% of the rural population had been landless; by 1936, that percentage had been reduced to 18%.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>By 1923, the extent of cultivated land surpassed the pre-war level. Innovation and rising productivity led to rapid growth of the economy, but it soon suffered from the effects of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>. Latvia showed signs of economic recovery, and the electorate had steadily moved toward the centre during the parliamentary period.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> On 15 May 1934, <a href="/wiki/1934_Latvian_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="1934 Latvian coup d'état">Ulmanis staged a bloodless coup</a>, establishing a nationalist dictatorship that lasted until 1940.<sup id="cite_ref-Country_profile_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Country_profile-58">[58]</a></sup> After 1934, Ulmanis established <a href="/wiki/Government_corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Government corporation">government corporations</a> to buy up private firms with the aim of "Latvianising" the economy.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Latvia_in_World_War_II">Latvia in World War II</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latvia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Latvia in World War II">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       See also: <a href="/wiki/Soviet_occupation_of_Latvia_in_1940" title="Soviet occupation of Latvia in 1940">Soviet occupation of Latvia in 1940</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_occupation_of_Latvia_during_World_War_II" title="German occupation of Latvia during World War II">German occupation of Latvia during World War II</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust_in_Latvia" title="The Holocaust in Latvia">The Holocaust in Latvia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvian_partisans" title="Latvian partisans">Latvian partisans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Latvian_anti-Nazi_resistance_movement_1941%E2%80%9345" title="Latvian anti-Nazi resistance movement 1941–45">Latvian anti-Nazi resistance movement 1941–45</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Riga_1940_Soviet_Army.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Riga_1940_Soviet_Army.jpg/220px-Riga_1940_Soviet_Army.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Riga_1940_Soviet_Army.jpg/330px-Riga_1940_Soviet_Army.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Riga_1940_Soviet_Army.jpg/440px-Riga_1940_Soviet_Army.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="566"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Riga_1940_Soviet_Army.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div><a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">Red Army</a> troops enter <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a> (1940).
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>Early in the morning of 24 August 1939, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> signed a 10-year non-aggression pact, called the <a href="/wiki/Molotov%E2%80%93Ribbentrop_Pact" title="Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact">Molotov–Ribbentrop Pact</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The pact contained a secret protocol, revealed only after Germany's defeat in 1945, according to which the states of Northern and Eastern Europe were divided into German and Soviet "<a href="/wiki/Sphere_of_influence" title="Sphere of influence">spheres of influence</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-mrtext_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mrtext-61">[61]</a></sup> In the north, Latvia, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> and Estonia were assigned to the Soviet sphere.<sup id="cite_ref-mrtext_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mrtext-61">[61]</a></sup> A week later, on 1 September 1939, Germany and on 17 September, the Soviet Union <a href="/wiki/Soviet_invasion_of_Poland" title="Soviet invasion of Poland">invaded Poland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PBBG_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBBG-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>32</span></sup> </p>
      <p>After the conclusion of the Molotov-Ribbentrop Pact, most of the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Germans" title="Baltic Germans">Baltic Germans</a> left Latvia by agreement between Ulmanis' government and Nazi Germany under the <a href="/wiki/Heim_ins_Reich" title="Heim ins Reich">Heim ins Reich</a> programme.<sup id="cite_ref-Latvia_in_WWII_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Latvia_in_WWII-63">[63]</a></sup> In total 50,000 Baltic Germans left by the deadline of December 1939, with 1,600 remaining to conclude business and 13,000 choosing to remain in Latvia.<sup id="cite_ref-Latvia_in_WWII_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Latvia_in_WWII-63">[63]</a></sup> Most of those who remained left for Germany in summer 1940, when a second resettlement scheme was agreed.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> The racially approved being resettled mainly in Poland, being given land and businesses in exchange for the money they had received from the sale of their previous assets.<sup id="cite_ref-PBBG_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBBG-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>46</span></sup> </p>
      <p>On 5 October 1939, Latvia was forced to accept a "mutual assistance" pact with the Soviet Union, granting the Soviets the right to station between 25,000 and 30,000 troops on Latvian territory.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> State administrators were liquidated and replaced by Soviet cadres.<sup id="cite_ref-wettig20_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wettig20-66">[66]</a></sup> Elections were held with single pro-Soviet candidates listed for many positions. The resulting people's assembly immediately requested admission into the USSR, which the Soviet Union granted.<sup id="cite_ref-wettig20_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wettig20-66">[66]</a></sup> Latvia, then a puppet government, was headed by <a href="/wiki/Augusts_Kirhen%C5%A1teins" title="Augusts Kirhenšteins">Augusts Kirhenšteins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> The Soviet Union incorporated Latvia on 5 August 1940, as The <i><a href="/wiki/Latvian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Latvian Soviet Socialist Republic">Latvian Soviet Socialist Republic</a>.</i> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L19397,_Lettland,_Riga,_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fung_der_deutschen_Soldaten.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L19397%2C_Lettland%2C_Riga%2C_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fung_der_deutschen_Soldaten.jpg/220px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L19397%2C_Lettland%2C_Riga%2C_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fung_der_deutschen_Soldaten.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L19397%2C_Lettland%2C_Riga%2C_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fung_der_deutschen_Soldaten.jpg/330px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L19397%2C_Lettland%2C_Riga%2C_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fung_der_deutschen_Soldaten.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L19397%2C_Lettland%2C_Riga%2C_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fung_der_deutschen_Soldaten.jpg/440px-Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L19397%2C_Lettland%2C_Riga%2C_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fung_der_deutschen_Soldaten.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="540"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Bundesarchiv_Bild_183-L19397,_Lettland,_Riga,_Begr%C3%BC%C3%9Fung_der_deutschen_Soldaten.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>German soldiers enter Riga, July 1941
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The Soviets dealt harshly with their <a href="/wiki/Enemy_of_the_people" title="Enemy of the people">opponents</a>&nbsp;– prior to <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Operation Barbarossa</a>, in less than a year, at least 34,250 Latvians were deported or killed.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Most were deported to Siberia where deaths were estimated at 40 percent, officers of the Latvian army being shot on the spot.<sup id="cite_ref-PBBG_62-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBBG-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>48</span></sup> </p>
      <p>On 22 June 1941 German troops attacked Soviet forces in Operation Barbarossa.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> There were some spontaneous uprisings by Latvians against the Red Army which helped the Germans. By 29 June <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a> was reached and with Soviet troops killed, captured or retreating, Latvia was left under the control of German forces by early July<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-PBBG_62-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBBG-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>78–96</span></sup> The occupation was followed immediately by SS <a href="/wiki/Einsatzgruppen" title="Einsatzgruppen">Einsatzgruppen</a> troops who were to act in accordance with the Nazi <a href="/wiki/Generalplan_Ost" title="Generalplan Ost">Generalplan Ost</a> which required the population of Latvia to be cut by 50 percent.<sup id="cite_ref-PBBG_62-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBBG-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>64</span></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PBBG_62-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBBG-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>56</span></sup> </p>
      <p>Under German occupation, Latvia was administered as part of <i><a href="/wiki/Reichskommissariat_Ostland" title="Reichskommissariat Ostland">Reichskommissariat Ostland</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Latvian paramilitary and <a href="/wiki/Arajs_Kommando" title="Arajs Kommando">Auxiliary Police</a> units established by the occupation authority participated in <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">the Holocaust</a> and other atrocities.<sup id="cite_ref-Country_profile_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Country_profile-58">[58]</a></sup> 30,000 Jews were shot in Latvia in the autumn of 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-PBBG_62-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBBG-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>127</span></sup> Another 30,000 Jews from the Riga ghetto were killed in the Rumbula Forest in November and December 1941, to reduce overpopulation in the ghetto and make room for more Jews being brought in from Germany and the West.<sup id="cite_ref-PBBG_62-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBBG-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>128</span></sup> There was a pause in fighting, apart from partisan activity, until after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Leningrad" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Leningrad">siege of Leningrad</a> ended in January 1944 and the Soviet troops advanced, entering Latvia in July and eventually capturing Riga on 13 October 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-PBBG_62-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBBG-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>271</span></sup> </p>
      <p>More than 200,000 Latvian citizens died during World War II, including approximately 75,000 Latvian <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> murdered during the Nazi occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-Country_profile_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Country_profile-58">[58]</a></sup> Latvian soldiers fought on both sides of the conflict, mainly on the German side, with 140,000 men in the <a href="/wiki/Latvian_Legion" title="Latvian Legion">Latvian Legion</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Waffen-SS" title="Waffen-SS">Waffen-SS</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/308th_Rifle_Division_(Soviet_Union)" title="308th Rifle Division (Soviet Union)">308th Latvian Rifle Division</a> was formed by the Red Army in 1944. On occasions, especially in 1944, opposing Latvian troops faced each other in battle.<sup id="cite_ref-PBBG_62-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBBG-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>299</span></sup> </p>
      <p>In the 23rd block of the Vorverker cemetery, a monument was erected after the Second World War for the people of Latvia, who had died in <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCbeck" title="Lübeck">Lübeck</a> from 1945 to 1950. </p> 
      <h3><span id="Soviet_era_.281940.E2.80.931941.2C_1944.E2.80.931991.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_era_(1940–1941,_1944–1991)">Soviet era (1940–1941, 1944–1991)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latvia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Soviet era (1940–1941, 1944–1991)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_Latvia_by_Soviet_Union_1944%E2%80%931945" class="mw-redirect" title="Occupation of Latvia by Soviet Union 1944–1945">Occupation of Latvia by Soviet Union 1944–1945</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latvian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Latvian Soviet Socialist Republic">Latvian Soviet Socialist Republic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stalinism" title="Stalinism">Stalinism</a>
      </div> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Liepaja_December_1941_massacres_01.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Liepaja_December_1941_massacres_01.jpeg/220px-Liepaja_December_1941_massacres_01.jpeg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Liepaja_December_1941_massacres_01.jpeg/330px-Liepaja_December_1941_massacres_01.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Liepaja_December_1941_massacres_01.jpeg/440px-Liepaja_December_1941_massacres_01.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="319"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Liepaja_December_1941_massacres_01.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Latvian-Jewish women and children photographed before <a href="/wiki/Liep%C4%81ja_massacres" title="Liepāja massacres">being murdered at Liepaja</a> in December 1941.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In 1944, when Soviet military advances reached Latvia, heavy fighting took place in Latvia between German and Soviet troops, which ended in another German defeat. In the course of the war, both occupying forces conscripted Latvians into their armies, in this way increasing the loss of the nation's "live resources". In 1944, part of the Latvian territory once more came under Soviet control. The Soviets immediately began to reinstate the Soviet system. After the German surrender, it became clear that Soviet forces were there to stay, and <a href="/wiki/Latvian_national_partisans" class="mw-redirect" title="Latvian national partisans">Latvian national partisans</a>, soon joined by some who had collaborated with the Germans, began to fight against the new occupier.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Anywhere from 120,000 to as many as 300,000 Latvians took refuge from the Soviet army by fleeing to Germany and Sweden.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-74">[74]</a></sup> Most sources count 200,000 to 250,000 refugees leaving Latvia, with perhaps as many as 80,000 to 100,000 of them recaptured by the Soviets or, during few months immediately after the end of war,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> returned by the West.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The Soviets reoccupied the country in 1944–1945, and further deportations followed as the country was <a href="/wiki/Collectivization_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Collectivization in the Soviet Union">collectivised</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sovietisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Sovietisation">Sovieticised</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Country_profile_58-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Country_profile-58">[58]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>On 25 March 1949, 43,000 rural residents ("<a href="/wiki/Kulak" title="Kulak">kulaks</a>") and Latvian nationalists were deported to Siberia in a sweeping <a href="/wiki/Operation_Priboi" title="Operation Priboi">Operation Priboi</a> in all three <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic states</a>, which was carefully planned and approved in Moscow already on 29 January 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> This operation had the desired effect of reducing the anti Soviet partisan activity.<sup id="cite_ref-PBBG_62-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PBBG-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:<span>326</span></sup> Between 136,000 and 190,000 Latvians, depending on the sources, were imprisoned or deported to Soviet concentration camps (the <a href="/wiki/Gulag" title="Gulag">Gulag</a>) in the post war years, from 1945 to 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Some managed to escape arrest and joined the partisans.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Shack_from_Gulag_-_Museum_of_the_Occupation_of_Latvia.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Shack_from_Gulag_-_Museum_of_the_Occupation_of_Latvia.JPG/220px-Shack_from_Gulag_-_Museum_of_the_Occupation_of_Latvia.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Shack_from_Gulag_-_Museum_of_the_Occupation_of_Latvia.JPG/330px-Shack_from_Gulag_-_Museum_of_the_Occupation_of_Latvia.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Shack_from_Gulag_-_Museum_of_the_Occupation_of_Latvia.JPG/440px-Shack_from_Gulag_-_Museum_of_the_Occupation_of_Latvia.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1944"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Shack_from_Gulag_-_Museum_of_the_Occupation_of_Latvia.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Reconstruction of a <a href="/wiki/Gulag" title="Gulag">Gulag</a> shack in the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_the_Occupation_of_Latvia" title="Museum of the Occupation of Latvia">Museum of the Occupation of Latvia</a>, Riga
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>In the post-war period, Latvia was made to adopt Soviet farming methods. Rural areas were forced into <a href="/wiki/Collectivization_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Collectivization in the Soviet Union">collectivization</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> An extensive program to impose <a href="/wiki/Bilingualism" class="mw-redirect" title="Bilingualism">bilingualism</a> was initiated in Latvia, limiting the use of Latvian language in official uses in favor of using Russian as the main language. All of the minority schools (Jewish, Polish, Belarusian, Estonian, Lithuanian) were closed down leaving only two media of instructions in the schools: Latvian and Russian.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> An influx of new colonists, including laborers, administrators, military personnel and their dependents from Russia and other Soviet republics started. By 1959 about 400,000 Russian settlers arrived and the ethnic Latvian population had fallen to 62%.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Since Latvia had maintained a well-developed infrastructure and educated specialists, Moscow decided to base some of the Soviet Union's most advanced manufacturing in Latvia. New industry was created in Latvia, including a major <a href="/wiki/Riga_Autobus_Factory" title="Riga Autobus Factory">machinery factory RAF</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jelgava" title="Jelgava">Jelgava</a>, electrotechnical factories in <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a>, chemical factories in <a href="/wiki/Daugavpils" title="Daugavpils">Daugavpils</a>, <a href="/wiki/Valmiera" title="Valmiera">Valmiera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Olaine" title="Olaine">Olaine</a>—and some food and oil processing plants.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Latvia manufactured trains, ships, minibuses, mopeds, telephones, radios and hi-fi systems, electrical and diesel engines, textiles, furniture, clothing, bags and luggage, shoes, musical instruments, home appliances, watches, tools and equipment, aviation and agricultural equipment and long list of other goods. Latvia had its own film industry and musical records factory (LPs). However, there were not enough people to operate the newly built factories.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> To maintain and expand industrial production, skilled workers were migrating from all over the Soviet Union, decreasing the proportion of ethnic Latvians in the republic.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> The population of Latvia reached its peak in 1990 at just under 2.7 million people. </p>
      <p>In late 2018 the National Archives of Latvia released a full alphabetical index of some 10,000 people recruited as agents or informants by the Soviet KGB. 'The publication, which followed two decades of public debate and the passage of a special law, revealed the names, code names, birthplaces and other data on active and former KGB agents as of 1991, the year Latvia regained its independence from the Soviet Union.'<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Restoration_of_independence_in_1991">Restoration of independence in 1991</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latvia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Restoration of independence in 1991">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3> 
      <div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">
       Further information: <a href="/wiki/Singing_Revolution" title="Singing Revolution">Singing Revolution</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Way" title="Baltic Way">Baltic Way</a>, and <a href="/wiki/On_the_Restoration_of_Independence_of_the_Republic_of_Latvia" title="On the Restoration of Independence of the Republic of Latvia">On the Restoration of Independence of the Republic of Latvia</a>
      </div> 
      <p>In the second half of the 1980s, Soviet leader <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a> started to introduce political and economic reforms in the Soviet Union that were called <a href="/wiki/Glasnost" title="Glasnost">glasnost</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perestroika" title="Perestroika">perestroika</a>. In the summer of 1987, the first large demonstrations were held in Riga at the <a href="/wiki/Freedom_Monument" title="Freedom Monument">Freedom Monument</a>—a symbol of independence. In the summer of 1988, a national movement, coalescing in the <a href="/wiki/Popular_Front_of_Latvia" title="Popular Front of Latvia">Popular Front of Latvia</a>, was opposed by the <a href="/wiki/International_Front_of_the_Working_People_of_Latvia" title="International Front of the Working People of Latvia">Interfront</a>. The Latvian SSR, along with the other <a href="/wiki/Baltic_states" title="Baltic states">Baltic Republics</a> was allowed greater autonomy, and in 1988, the old pre-war <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Latvia" title="Flag of Latvia">Flag of Latvia</a> flew again, replacing the Soviet Latvian flag as the official flag in 1990.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In 1989, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Soviet_of_the_USSR" class="mw-redirect" title="Supreme Soviet of the USSR">Supreme Soviet of the USSR</a> adopted a resolution on the <i><a href="/wiki/Occupation_of_the_Baltic_states" title="Occupation of the Baltic states">Occupation of the Baltic states</a></i>, in which it declared the occupation "not in accordance with law", and not the "will of the Soviet people". Pro-independence Popular Front of Latvia candidates gained a two-thirds majority in the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Council_of_the_Republic_of_Latvia" title="Supreme Council of the Republic of Latvia">Supreme Council</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1990_Latvian_Supreme_Soviet_election" title="1990 Latvian Supreme Soviet election">March 1990 democratic elections</a>. On 4 May 1990, the Supreme Council adopted the <a href="/wiki/Declaration_on_the_Restoration_of_Independence_of_the_Republic_of_Latvia" class="mw-redirect" title="Declaration on the Restoration of Independence of the Republic of Latvia">Declaration on the Restoration of Independence of the Republic of Latvia</a>, and the Latvian SSR was renamed Republic of Latvia.<sup id="cite_ref-Imagining_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Imagining-87">[87]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>However, the central power in Moscow continued to regard Latvia as a Soviet republic in 1990 and 1991. In January 1991, Soviet political and military forces tried unsuccessfully to overthrow the Republic of Latvia authorities by occupying the central publishing house in Riga and establishing a Committee of National Salvation to usurp governmental functions. During the transitional period, Moscow maintained many central Soviet state authorities in Latvia.<sup id="cite_ref-Imagining_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Imagining-87">[87]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>In spite of this, 73% of all Latvian residents confirmed their strong support for <a href="/wiki/Independence" title="Independence">independence</a> on 3 March 1991, in a non-binding advisory <a href="/wiki/Referendum" title="Referendum">referendum</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The Popular Front of Latvia advocated that all permanent residents be eligible for Latvian citizenship, helping to sway many ethnic Russians to vote for independence. However, universal citizenship for all permanent residents was not adopted. Instead, citizenship was granted to persons who had been citizens of Latvia at the day of loss of independence at 1940 as well as their descendants. As a consequence, the majority of ethnic non-Latvians did not receive Latvian citizenship since neither they nor their parents had ever been citizens of Latvia, becoming <a href="/wiki/Non-citizens_(Latvia)" title="Non-citizens (Latvia)">non-citizens</a> or citizens of other former Soviet republics. By 2011, more than half of non-citizens had taken <a href="/wiki/Naturalization" title="Naturalization">naturalization</a> exams and received Latvian citizenship. Still, today there are 290,660 non-citizens in Latvia, which represent 14.1% of the population. They have <a href="/wiki/Statelessness" title="Statelessness">no citizenship of any country</a>, and cannot vote in Latvia.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>The Republic of Latvia declared the end of the transitional period and restored full independence on 21 August 1991, in the aftermath of the failed <a href="/wiki/1991_Soviet_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="1991 Soviet coup d'état attempt">Soviet coup attempt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FINemb_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FINemb-4">[4]</a></sup> </p> 
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a href="/wiki/File:Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_(081).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg/220px-Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg/330px-Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg/440px-Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_%28081%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="521" data-file-height="310"></a> 
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Tratado_de_Lisboa_13_12_2007_(081).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a>
         </div>Latvia became a member of the European Union in 2004 and signed the <a href="/wiki/Lisbon_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Lisbon Treaty">Lisbon Treaty</a> in 2007.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div> 
      <p>The <a href="/wiki/Saeima" title="Saeima">Saeima</a>, Latvia's parliament, was again elected in 1993. Russia ended its military presence by completing its troop withdrawal in 1994 and shutting down the <a href="/wiki/Skrunda-1" title="Skrunda-1">Skrunda-1</a> radar station in 1998. The major goals of Latvia in the 1990s, to join <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> and the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, were achieved in 2004. The <a href="/wiki/NATO_Summit_2006" class="mw-redirect" title="NATO Summit 2006">NATO Summit 2006</a> was held in Riga.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> </p>
      <p>Language and citizenship laws have been opposed by many <a href="/wiki/Geographical_distribution_of_Russian_speakers" title="Geographical distribution of Russian speakers">Russophones</a>. <a href="/wiki/Citizenship" title="Citizenship">Citizenship</a> was not automatically extended to former Soviet citizens who settled during the Soviet occupation, or to their offspring. Children born to non-nationals after the reestablishment of independence are automatically entitled to citizenship. Approximately 72% of Latvian citizens are Latvian, while 20% are Russian; less than 1% of non-citizens are Latvian, while 71% are Russian.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> The government denationalized private property confiscated by the Soviets, returning it or compensating the owners for it, and <a href="/wiki/Privatization" title="Privatization">privatized</a> most state-owned industries, reintroducing the <a href="/wiki/Latvian_lats" title="Latvian lats">prewar currency</a>. Albeit having experienced a difficult transition to a liberal economy and its re-orientation toward Western Europe, Latvia is one of the fastest growing economies in the European Union. In 2014, <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a> was the <a href="/wiki/European_Capital_of_Culture" title="European Capital of Culture">European Capital of Culture</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Euro" title="Euro">euro</a> was introduced as the currency of the country and a Latvian was named vice-president of the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a>. In 2015 Latvia held the presidency of <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Council of the European Union">Council of the European Union</a>. Big European events have been celebrated in <a href="/wiki/Riga" title="Riga">Riga</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest_2003" title="Eurovision Song Contest 2003">Eurovision Song Contest 2003</a> and the <a href="/wiki/27th_European_Film_Awards" title="27th European Film Awards">European Film Awards 2014</a>. On 1 July 2016, Latvia became a member of the OECD.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> </p> 
      <h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regional_timeline">Regional timeline</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latvia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Regional timeline">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
